Why God's Attributes Must Be Eternal
Refutes an objection against eternal divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school insists that God's attributes must be eternal and real, based on a refutation of an objection that compares God's self-praise to that of prophets.

Core Idea
God's self-praise in eternity proves His attributes are real, not future hopes.

This passage tackles a key objection against eternal attributes: that praising oneself for a future attribute might be acceptable. The author distinguishes between created beings and God to show why God's attributes must be present from pre-eternity. Understanding this distinction is central to the Maturidi defense of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the objection that is being refuted and the author's initial response.
Pay attention to the examples of Ibrahim and Muhammad.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author argues that for created beings, praise is based on future attainment. For God, who existed in pre-eternity, His self-praise must be based on attributes He already possesses.
3
Analyze the logical consequence
If God's attributes were not eternal, His self-praise would be like wishing or boasting about something not yet possessed—a sign of deficiency.
This is the heart of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that denying eternal attributes leads to absurdities, and hints at further proofs from the Quran.
Think about why this matters for the doctrine of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : هذا القدر في إبراهيم ومحمد وغيرهما من المخلوقين مستقيم، لأنهم لم يمتدحوا بما ليس لهم بل الله أخبر أنهم متى يوجدون ينالون هذه الفضيلة. فأما الله تعالى فهو كان موجودًا في الأزل ووصف نفسه بذلك وتمدح، لا أن أخبر أنه يصير كذلك، فلو لم تكن الصفة ثابتة لكان تمدحا بما سيصير موصوفا به، فإنه وهو للحال موجود غير مستحق لتلك المحمدة، فيصير كالمتمني ثبوت ما ليس بثابت له، والمتشرف بما لم يثبت له بعد والمحب أن يمدح بما لم يفعل بعد، وهذا كله أمارة النقص والحاجة والسفه. ولصحة مذهبنا دلائل أخر من كتاب الله تعالى تركنا ذكرها لأن الكلام في هذه المسألة قد طال جدا.
It was said to them: This amount (of description) is sound for Ibrahim, Muhammad, and other created beings, because they were not praised for what they do not have; rather Allah informed that whenever they exist they will attain this virtue. As for Allah the Exalted, He was existent in pre-eternity and described Himself with that and praised Himself, not that He informed that He would become such. So if the attribute were not established, it would be praising oneself for what one will become described with, while He in the present is existent and not deserving of that praise. Thus He would be like one who wishes for what is not established for him, and one who boasts of what is not yet established for him, and one who loves to be praised for what he has not yet done. All of this is a sign of deficiency, need, and foolishness. For the correctness of our school there are other proofs from the Book of Allah, but we have omitted mentioning them because the discussion on this issue has become very long.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection that was likely raised by opponents (perhaps the Mu'tazila) against the Maturidi view that the divine attributes (ṣifāt) are real and eternal. The objection claimed that praising oneself for an attribute would be like the case of Prophets Ibrahim and Muhammad, who were praised by Allah for future states they would attain. The author answers: For created beings, the praise is based on Allah's information that they will attain that virtue when they exist. But for Allah, He was already existent in pre-eternity (al-azal) and described Himself with those attributes, not that He would acquire them later. If the attribute were not actually present from eternity, then His self-praise would be like wishing for something not yet possessed or boasting about a future state, which are signs of deficiency (naqṣ), need (ḥājah), and foolishness (safah). Thus the attributes must be real and eternal. The author then notes that many other proofs from the Quran exist but are omitted due to length.

Key Terms
صفة (ṣifa)
الأزل (al-azal)
تمدح (tamaduḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that praising oneself for a future attribute is acceptable for God, just as it is for prophets.
Correction: For prophets, praise is based on Allah's information about a future state. For God, who existed eternally, self-praise must be based on attributes He already possesses, not future hopes.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence, according to the author, if God's attributes were not real in pre-eternity?
A) God would be like a created being.
B) God's praise of Himself would be a boast about something not yet possessed.
C) The attributes would be acquired later in time.
D) The prophets would not have virtue.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal self-description necessitates that His attributes are real and eternal, not future hopes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's self-praise is based on what He eternally is, not on what He might become, does your own self-image rely on future achievements rather than who you truly are before Allah right now? Will you stop seeking validation from what you hope to become and instead humble yourself before His eternal perfection?
?
The author likens praising for future attributes to foolishness. When you praise yourself for what you plan to do, are you not acting with the same deficiency that you know is impossible for God? How will you replace self-congratulation with gratitude for what Allah has already given you?
?
God's attributes are real and eternal; you cannot add to them or change them. Does your worship reflect that you serve a God who is complete and unchanging, or do you sometimes act as if He needs your deeds to become better? What would change in your prayer if you truly believed Allah's perfection is eternally complete without your actions?

One Act, Multiple Names: The Maturidi Doctrine of Divine Unity
Demonstrates how a single divine act receives multiple names according to effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single divine act can have multiple names based on its effects, and how this doctrine preserves the unity of Allah's essence and attribute.

Core Idea
Multiple names for God come from effects, not from changes in His essence.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a key Maturidi principle: a single act can receive different names depending on its outcomes. He first illustrates this with everyday human actions, then applies it to Allah, arguing that the diversity of divine names (like 'Life-giver' and 'Death-giver') arises from the diversity of created effects, not from any multiplicity in Allah's essence or act. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the flow from general principle to human examples to divine application.
The examples from moving a hand are meant to make the abstract principle concrete.
2
Identify the principle
Find the sentence that states the general principle: 'a single act has its names differing according to its effects and intelligibles.' Understand that 'intelligibles' (ma'qulat) refers to mental concepts or aspects.
3
Trace the examples
The author gives a series of examples from human action: moving the hand can be called causing pain, breaking, cutting, or killing depending on the effect. Notice how the act itself is one but the name changes.
These examples establish the pattern before applying it to Allah.
4
Apply to Allah and conclude
Read how the author applies this to Allah: He is a doer by a single act, just as He is powerful by a single power. The different names (like Muhyi, Mumit) are due to different effects, not any change in His essence. Then note the apologetic conclusion about truth and falsehood.
The final remarks highlight that this doctrine is distinctive to the Maturidi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلموا - رحمكم الله - أن الفعل الواحد تختلف أساميه باختلاف آثاره ومعقولاته فمهما كان الحاصل به حياة يسمى إحياء، ومهما كان الحاصل به موتا يسمى إماتة ومهما كان الحاصل به حركة يسمى تحريكا ومهما كان الحاصل به سكونا يسمى تسكينا، فإن من حرك يده يسمى ذلك منه فعلا، فإن صار ذلك سببا من حيث العادة لوجود ألم في شخص سمي إيلاما وإن كان سببا لحصول انكسار يسمى كسرا، وإن كان سببا لحصول انقطاع في محل يسمى قطعا، وإن صار سببا لحصول انزهاق الروح من محل يسمى قتلا، فعلى هذا كان الله تعالى عندنا فاعلا بفعل واحد كما هو قادر بقدرة واحدة، عالم بعلم واحد. واختلاف الأسامي عليه لاختلاف آثاره ومفعولاته لا لاختلافه في ذاته، وتعدد أساميه وكثرتها بتعدد مفعولاته وكثرتها، لا لتعدد ذاته وكثرته. وقد طال الكلام في هذه المسألة وخرج عن المشروط في أول الكتاب، لأن أصحابنا في هذه المسألة متفردون وقد كثر - بعد انقراض محققيهم - من الخصوم المشاغبة والتشنيع، فأردنا أن نبين عن قوة الحق وضعف الباطل فإن الحق لا يضعف بقلة أعوانه، والباطل لا يقوى بكثرة حزبه وأنصاره، والله الموفق.
Then know - may Allah have mercy on you - that a single act has its names differing according to its effects and intelligibles. So whenever life results from it, it is called 'giving life' (iḥyā'), and whenever death results from it, it is called 'giving death' (imātah), and whenever movement results from it, it is called 'moving' (taḥrīk), and whenever stillness results from it, it is called 'stillifying' (taskīn). For if a person moves his hand, that is called from him an act. Then if it becomes a cause, by custom, for the existence of pain in a person, it is called 'causing pain' (īlām). And if it is a cause for the occurrence of breaking, it is called 'breaking' (kasr). And if it is a cause for the occurrence of cutting in a place, it is called 'cutting' (qaṭ'). And if it becomes a cause for the departure of the soul from a place, it is called 'killing' (qatl). On this basis, Allah the Exalted is, in our view, a doer (fā‘il) by a single act, just as He is powerful by a single power, knowing by a single knowledge. The difference of names for Him is due to the difference of His effects and acts (maf‘ūlāt), not due to any difference in His essence. And the multiplicity and abundance of His names is due to the multiplicity and abundance of His acts, not due to multiplicity and abundance in His essence. And the discussion on this issue has become long and has exceeded the condition set at the beginning of the book, because our companions (aṣḥābnā) are unique in this issue, and after the passing of their verifying scholars, the opponents have increased in disputation and denigration. So we wanted to clarify the strength of truth and the weakness of falsehood, for truth is not weakened by the fewness of its supporters, and falsehood does not become strong by the multitude of its party and supporters. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage presents a key Maturidi doctrine on the unity of the divine act (fi‘l wāḥid) and the multiplicity of divine names. The author begins by explaining a general principle: a single act can receive different names depending on its effects and conceptual aspects (ma‘qūlāt). He gives examples from created acts: moving one's hand can be called 'causing pain' if it leads to pain, 'breaking' if it breaks something, 'cutting' if it cuts, 'killing' if it causes death. These names vary while the act itself remains one. The author then applies this to Allah: Allah is a doer (fā‘il) by a single act, just as He is powerful by one power and knowing by one knowledge. The differences in His names are due to the differences in His effects (āthār) and acts (maf‘ūlāt), not due to any change or multiplicity in His essence or attribute. For example, He is called 'Muhyī' (Life-giver) when life is created, 'Mumīt' (Death-giver) when death is created, etc. This refutes the view that God has multiple acts or that His attributes are multiple in essence. The author then concludes by noting that this issue is peculiar to the Maturidi school (aṣḥābnā) and that after the early verifying scholars passed away, opponents raised many objections. The purpose of the discussion is to demonstrate the strength of truth and weakness of falsehood, emphasizing that truth does not weaken due to few supporters.

Key Terms
فعل واحد (fi‘l wāḥid)
أثر (athar)
مفعول (maf‘ūl)
فاعل (fā‘il)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The multiplicity of God's names implies multiplicity in His essence or attributes.
Correction: The author clarifies that the multiplicity of names is solely due to the multiplicity of effects (maf‘ūlāt), not due to any multiplicity in His essence or act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a single act receive multiple names?
A) Because the act itself changes.
B) Because the essence of the doer changes.
C) Because the effects and conceptual aspects differ.
D) Because the names are arbitrary.

Key Takeaway
A single divine act gives rise to many names due to diverse effects; this preserves God's unity and avoids multiplicity in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names such as al-Muhyī or al-Mumīt, do you truly believe that these arise from one undivided act of His, or do you unconsciously imagine multiple, separate acts? Examine your heart: is your understanding of tawḥīd deep enough to see unity behind all His names?
?
Consider your own actions: a single word from you can heal or hurt, build or destroy. How often do you reflect that your one act has multiple consequences, and that judgment will be based on their effects? Will you let this lesson transform how you speak and act today?
?
The author ends by saying truth is not weakened by few supporters and falsehood not strengthened by many. In your life, where do you follow the majority at the expense of truth? Are you willing to stand alone for a truth you know, even if the crowd opposes it?

Divine Names and Acts: The Maturidi Conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes on divine attributes, affirming diversity of names due to acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the diversity of divine names is due to diversity of acts, not essence, and how this affirms tawhid in Maturidi theology.

Core Idea
God's many names point to His many acts, not a many-layered essence.

Al-Nasafi concludes his discussion on divine attributes with a powerful affirmation: God acts with one act, knows with one knowledge, yet His names are many. This might seem contradictory—how can one essence have multiple names? The passage resolves this by distinguishing between the divine essence (which is one) and divine effects (which are many). Before we read, note that this conclusion also addresses why the Maturidi school uniquely emphasizes this point against opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let al-Nasafi's own wording establish the point before we dissect it.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice how the diversity of names is attributed to the diversity of effects and acts, not to any change or multiplicity in God's essence. This is the core of the Maturidi position.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Al-Nasafi states he went into detail because opponents increased after the passing of Maturidi verifiers. This shows the polemical context and why clarity on this point matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى هذا كان الله تعالى عندنا فاعلا بفعل واحد كما هو قادر بقدرة واحدة، عالم بعلم واحد. واختلاف الأسامي عليه لاختلاف آثاره ومفعولاته لا لاختلافه في ذاته، وتعدد أساميه وكثرتها بتعدد مفعولاته وكثرتها ، لا لتعدد ذاته وكثرته. وقد طال الكلام في هذه المسألة وخرج (۱) عن المشروط في أول الكتاب، لأن أصحابنا في هذه المسألة متفردون وقد كثر - بعد انقراض محققيهم ــ من الخصوم المشاغبة والتشنيع، فأردنا أن نبين عن قوة الحق وضعف الباطل فإن الحق لا يضعف بقلة أعوانه، والباطل لا يقوى بكثرة حزبه وأنصاره(۲)، والله الموفق.
Thus, according to us, God Almighty is a doer with one act just as He is powerful with one power, knowing with one knowledge. The diversity of names upon Him is due to the diversity of His effects and acts, not due to diversity in His essence. The multiplicity of His names and their abundance is due to the multiplicity of His acts and their abundance, not due to multiplicity of His essence and its abundance. And the discussion has been lengthy in this issue and has gone beyond the condition set at the beginning of the book, because our companions are unique in this issue, and after the passing of their verifiers, the opponents have increased in objection and vilification. So we wanted to show the strength of truth and the weakness of falsehood, for truth is not weakened by the scarcity of its supporters, and falsehood is not strengthened by the abundance of its party and supporters. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark by al-Nasafi on the topic of divine attributes and actions. He reaffirms the Maturidi position that God is a doer (fā‘il) with one act (fi‘l wāḥid), just as He is powerful with one power and knowing with one knowledge. The diversity of names (asmā’) attributed to God—such as Creator, Provider, etc.—is due to the diversity of His effects and acts, not due to any diversity in His essence. The multiplicity of names corresponds to the multiplicity of acts, not to any multiplicity in the divine essence. Al-Nasafi then notes that the discussion has been lengthy and has exceeded the constraints set at the beginning of the book. He explains that the Maturidi scholars are unique in this matter, and after the passing of their rigorous verifiers, opponents have increased their objections and vilifications. The author's purpose is to demonstrate the strength of truth and the weakness of falsehood, because truth is not weakened by few supporters, nor falsehood strengthened by many. He concludes by invoking God's guidance.

Key Terms
فاعل
فعل واحد
قادر
قدرة واحدة
عالم
علم واحد
أسامي
آثار
مفعولات
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why are there multiple names for God?
A) Because God's essence is multiple
B) Because of the multiplicity of His acts and effects
C) Because the names are metaphors without real reference
D) Because God changes over time

Key Takeaway
The diversity of God's names reflects the diversity of His acts, not any multiplicity in His one essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the many names of Allah, like Ar-Razzaq or Al-Khaliq, do you see them as reminders of His acts in your life today? How often do you pause to thank Him for a specific act of provision or creation? Write one concrete act of His that you experienced today and call upon Him using the corresponding name.
?
Al-Nasafi defends the truth even when its supporters are few. Is there a truth you know but remain silent about because it's unpopular? Reflect on one area of your faith or morality where you compromise due to social pressure, and commit to speaking or acting on that truth this week.
